Phase II Variables (polity-based)[edit]

Institutional Variables[edit]

♠ RA ♣ Edward A L Turner ♥

♠ Expert ♣ ♥

♠ Editor ♣ ♥

Limits on Power of the Chief Executive[edit]

Legal (formal) limits[edit]

♠ Executive power is separate or independent from military ♣ absent ♥ The Parthians were "a military aristocracy, which found it most convenient to rule not directly but through various kinds of authorities". This meant "while provincial administration was extensive, central government was scant."[613] "The Parthian government consisted of the king and the royal court, in addition to the Council of the Nobles (the Mahistan)"[614], which made use of trained Greek scholars.[615] The Mahistan (Council of Nobles[616] and priests[617]) "was similar to the Roman Senate, its members being responsible for drawing laws and enacting them."[618] These were two different councils, one for kinsmen and one for magi.[619] The various parts of the Parthian realm were ruled "by great noble families, allegedly seven in all, such as the Surens, Karens, and Mihrans. These families commanded detachments of the army in battle and wielded semi-dependent suzerainty in their fiefdoms. The 'king of kings' was such in fact as well as in title, for he dealt apparently as primus inter pares with powerful lords."[620] "The court system of the Arsacids was copied from the Achaemenid model, being staffed by many offices called Diwans, responsible for record-keeping, communication, budgeting, and taxation. These were headed by their respective Dibirs who were all responsible to a first-minister, a member of the nobility as mentioned before."[621] "It seems, however, that the Parthians, much like their Achaemenid predecessors, did not have one single capital and moved from city to city along with their administration."[622] Would the Council of the Nobles, which drew and enacted the laws, and had an important role in the succession especially in the later Parthian era[623], have had to agree to the military use of force? One might speculate this was the case in theory but the king could do his own thing when opinion in the Mahistan was split.

(CC: I have changed above code to 'absent' based on the description. It sounds like military power was not separate from the executive, but that the political organization itself meant that there were several separate ruling families)

♠ Executive power is separate or independent from legislative ♣ inferred present ♥ The Parthians were "a military aristocracy, which found it most convenient to rule not directly but through various kinds of authorities". This meant "while provincial administration was extensive, central government was scant."[624] "The Parthian government consisted of the king and the royal court, in addition to the Council of the Nobles (the Mahistan)"[625], which made use of trained Greek scholars.[626] The Mahistan (Council of Nobles[627] and priests[628]) "was similar to the Roman Senate, its members being responsible for drawing laws and enacting them."[629] These were two different councils, one for kinsmen and one for magi.[630] The various parts of the Parthian realm were ruled "by great noble families, allegedly seven in all, such as the Surens, Karens, and Mihrans. These families commanded detachments of the army in battle and wielded semi-dependent suzerainty in their fiefdoms. The 'king of kings' was such in fact as well as in title, for he dealt apparently as primus inter pares with powerful lords."[631] "The court system of the Arsacids was copied from the Achaemenid model, being staffed by many offices called Diwans, responsible for record-keeping, communication, budgeting, and taxation. These were headed by their respective Dibirs who were all responsible to a first-minister, a member of the nobility as mentioned before."[632] "It seems, however, that the Parthians, much like their Achaemenid predecessors, did not have one single capital and moved from city to city along with their administration."[633] The Council of the Nobles drew and enacted the laws. In any case, most lawmaking would have been done at local levels by the autonomous cities and vassal states.
♠ Executive power is separate or independent from judiciary ♣ inferred present ♥ The Parthians were "a military aristocracy, which found it most convenient to rule not directly but through various kinds of authorities". This meant "while provincial administration was extensive, central government was scant."[634] "The Parthian government consisted of the king and the royal court, in addition to the Council of the Nobles (the Mahistan)"[635], which made use of trained Greek scholars.[636] The Mahistan (Council of Nobles[637] and priests[638]) "was similar to the Roman Senate, its members being responsible for drawing laws and enacting them."[639] These were two different councils, one for kinsmen and one for magi.[640] The various parts of the Parthian realm were ruled "by great noble families, allegedly seven in all, such as the Surens, Karens, and Mihrans. These families commanded detachments of the army in battle and wielded semi-dependent suzerainty in their fiefdoms. The 'king of kings' was such in fact as well as in title, for he dealt apparently as primus inter pares with powerful lords."[641] "The court system of the Arsacids was copied from the Achaemenid model, being staffed by many offices called Diwans, responsible for record-keeping, communication, budgeting, and taxation. These were headed by their respective Dibirs who were all responsible to a first-minister, a member of the nobility as mentioned before."[642] "It seems, however, that the Parthians, much like their Achaemenid predecessors, did not have one single capital and moved from city to city along with their administration."[643] This seems likely given the extent of local autonomy granted.

Legal (formal) freedoms[edit]

♠ Right to petition executive ♣ inferred present ♥ The Parthians were "a military aristocracy, which found it most convenient to rule not directly but through various kinds of authorities". This meant "while provincial administration was extensive, central government was scant."[644] "The Parthian government consisted of the king and the royal court, in addition to the Council of the Nobles (the Mahistan)"[645], which made use of trained Greek scholars.[646] The Mahistan (Council of Nobles[647] and priests[648]) "was similar to the Roman Senate, its members being responsible for drawing laws and enacting them."[649] These were two different councils, one for kinsmen and one for magi.[650] The various parts of the Parthian realm were ruled "by great noble families, allegedly seven in all, such as the Surens, Karens, and Mihrans. These families commanded detachments of the army in battle and wielded semi-dependent suzerainty in their fiefdoms. The 'king of kings' was such in fact as well as in title, for he dealt apparently as primus inter pares with powerful lords."[651] "The court system of the Arsacids was copied from the Achaemenid model, being staffed by many offices called Diwans, responsible for record-keeping, communication, budgeting, and taxation. These were headed by their respective Dibirs who were all responsible to a first-minister, a member of the nobility as mentioned before."[652] "It seems, however, that the Parthians, much like their Achaemenid predecessors, did not have one single capital and moved from city to city along with their administration."[653] Given the high level of local autonomy and lack of central government it would not have been necessary to petition the executive. There may have been the right to petition the executive within the autonomous cities and areas within the Parthian Empire, for example Greek-run Seleucia-on-the-Tigris. The Seleucid Empire is coded inferred present for this variable.
♠ Critique of executive by government ♣ inferred present ♥ The Parthians were "a military aristocracy, which found it most convenient to rule not directly but through various kinds of authorities". This meant "while provincial administration was extensive, central government was scant."[654] "The Parthian government consisted of the king and the royal court, in addition to the Council of the Nobles (the Mahistan)"[655], which made use of trained Greek scholars.[656] The Mahistan (Council of Nobles[657] and priests[658]) "was similar to the Roman Senate, its members being responsible for drawing laws and enacting them."[659] These were two different councils, one for kinsmen and one for magi.[660] The various parts of the Parthian realm were ruled "by great noble families, allegedly seven in all, such as the Surens, Karens, and Mihrans. These families commanded detachments of the army in battle and wielded semi-dependent suzerainty in their fiefdoms. The 'king of kings' was such in fact as well as in title, for he dealt apparently as primus inter pares with powerful lords."[661] "The court system of the Arsacids was copied from the Achaemenid model, being staffed by many offices called Diwans, responsible for record-keeping, communication, budgeting, and taxation. These were headed by their respective Dibirs who were all responsible to a first-minister, a member of the nobility as mentioned before."[662] "It seems, however, that the Parthians, much like their Achaemenid predecessors, did not have one single capital and moved from city to city along with their administration."[663] The Mahistan was part of the government and we can infer that it could critique the executive since it had the powers to draw and enact law and especially in the later Parthian era choose the new king.[664]
♠ Critique of executive by non-government ♣ inferred present ♥ The Parthians were "a military aristocracy, which found it most convenient to rule not directly but through various kinds of authorities". This meant "while provincial administration was extensive, central government was scant."[665] "The Parthian government consisted of the king and the royal court, in addition to the Council of the Nobles (the Mahistan)"[666], which made use of trained Greek scholars.[667] The Mahistan (Council of Nobles[668] and priests[669]) "was similar to the Roman Senate, its members being responsible for drawing laws and enacting them."[670] These were two different councils, one for kinsmen and one for magi.[671] The various parts of the Parthian realm were ruled "by great noble families, allegedly seven in all, such as the Surens, Karens, and Mihrans. These families commanded detachments of the army in battle and wielded semi-dependent suzerainty in their fiefdoms. The 'king of kings' was such in fact as well as in title, for he dealt apparently as primus inter pares with powerful lords."[672] "The court system of the Arsacids was copied from the Achaemenid model, being staffed by many offices called Diwans, responsible for record-keeping, communication, budgeting, and taxation. These were headed by their respective Dibirs who were all responsible to a first-minister, a member of the nobility as mentioned before."[673] "It seems, however, that the Parthians, much like their Achaemenid predecessors, did not have one single capital and moved from city to city along with their administration."[674] The powerful families that constituted the Parthian military aristocracy would have been able to critique the executive, principally, but presumably not only, through the Mahistan.

Power distributed[edit]

♠ Sanction of executive by government ♣ inferred absent ♥ The Parthians were "a military aristocracy, which found it most convenient to rule not directly but through various kinds of authorities". This meant "while provincial administration was extensive, central government was scant."[675] "The Parthian government consisted of the king and the royal court, in addition to the Council of the Nobles (the Mahistan)"[676], which made use of trained Greek scholars.[677] The Mahistan (Council of Nobles[678] and priests[679]) "was similar to the Roman Senate, its members being responsible for drawing laws and enacting them."[680] These were two different councils, one for kinsmen and one for magi.[681] The various parts of the Parthian realm were ruled "by great noble families, allegedly seven in all, such as the Surens, Karens, and Mihrans. These families commanded detachments of the army in battle and wielded semi-dependent suzerainty in their fiefdoms. The 'king of kings' was such in fact as well as in title, for he dealt apparently as primus inter pares with powerful lords."[682] "The court system of the Arsacids was copied from the Achaemenid model, being staffed by many offices called Diwans, responsible for record-keeping, communication, budgeting, and taxation. These were headed by their respective Dibirs who were all responsible to a first-minister, a member of the nobility as mentioned before."[683] "It seems, however, that the Parthians, much like their Achaemenid predecessors, did not have one single capital and moved from city to city along with their administration."[684] There was little in the way of Parthian government other than a court and a Council of Nobles. The Parthian aristocracy reflected in the Mahistan (Council of Nobles) had the power to draw and enact law and especially in the later Parthian era appoint the king's successor.[685] However, the sources do not say that the Mahistan could punish the king. The culture implied by a hypothetical Council punishing a king is at odds with the observation the Parthian king was primus inter pares with the powerful lords. Disagreements between the Parthian king and the nobles would more likely have broken down into fighting rather than ended in a legal action which would have attempted to re-establish the power of the lords; Parthian history particularly from the 1st century BCE charts the existence of open conflicts between rulers and aristocrats.[686]
♠ Sanction of executive by non-government ♣ inferred absent ♥ The Parthians were "a military aristocracy, which found it most convenient to rule not directly but through various kinds of authorities". This meant "while provincial administration was extensive, central government was scant."[687] "The Parthian government consisted of the king and the royal court, in addition to the Council of the Nobles (the Mahistan)"[688], which made use of trained Greek scholars.[689] The Mahistan (Council of Nobles[690] and priests[691]) "was similar to the Roman Senate, its members being responsible for drawing laws and enacting them."[692] These were two different councils, one for kinsmen and one for magi.[693] The various parts of the Parthian realm were ruled "by great noble families, allegedly seven in all, such as the Surens, Karens, and Mihrans. These families commanded detachments of the army in battle and wielded semi-dependent suzerainty in their fiefdoms. The 'king of kings' was such in fact as well as in title, for he dealt apparently as primus inter pares with powerful lords."[694] "The court system of the Arsacids was copied from the Achaemenid model, being staffed by many offices called Diwans, responsible for record-keeping, communication, budgeting, and taxation. These were headed by their respective Dibirs who were all responsible to a first-minister, a member of the nobility as mentioned before."[695] "It seems, however, that the Parthians, much like their Achaemenid predecessors, did not have one single capital and moved from city to city along with their administration."[696] No reference to such a mechanism in the sources consulted. Given the high level of local autonomy, opposition to the executive was usually expressed through provincial revolts so the non-government (i.e. powerful families) did not need to sanction the executive to get their way.
♠ Constraint on executive by government ♣ inferred present ♥ The Parthians were "a military aristocracy, which found it most convenient to rule not directly but through various kinds of authorities". This meant "while provincial administration was extensive, central government was scant."[697] "The Parthian government consisted of the king and the royal court, in addition to the Council of the Nobles (the Mahistan)"[698], which made use of trained Greek scholars.[699] The Mahistan (Council of Nobles[700] and priests[701]) "was similar to the Roman Senate, its members being responsible for drawing laws and enacting them."[702] These were two different councils, one for kinsmen and one for magi.[703] The various parts of the Parthian realm were ruled "by great noble families, allegedly seven in all, such as the Surens, Karens, and Mihrans. These families commanded detachments of the army in battle and wielded semi-dependent suzerainty in their fiefdoms. The 'king of kings' was such in fact as well as in title, for he dealt apparently as primus inter pares with powerful lords."[704] "The court system of the Arsacids was copied from the Achaemenid model, being staffed by many offices called Diwans, responsible for record-keeping, communication, budgeting, and taxation. These were headed by their respective Dibirs who were all responsible to a first-minister, a member of the nobility as mentioned before."[705] "It seems, however, that the Parthians, much like their Achaemenid predecessors, did not have one single capital and moved from city to city along with their administration."[706] There was little in the way of Parthian central government to constrain but the Mahistan (Council of Nobles) which could draw and enact laws, and choose the king's successor particularly in the later Parthian era[707] was a constraint on the executive. Local government also proved to be a constraint: autonomy granted to Greek cities such as Seleucia on the Tigris "often the source of tensions and serious conflicts with Parthian rulers trying to restrain it."[708] The Parthians provided the autonomous city of Susa with its own charter.[709]
♠ Constraint on executive by non-government ♣ inferred present ♥ The Parthians were "a military aristocracy, which found it most convenient to rule not directly but through various kinds of authorities". This meant "while provincial administration was extensive, central government was scant."[710] "The Parthian government consisted of the king and the royal court, in addition to the Council of the Nobles (the Mahistan)"[711], which made use of trained Greek scholars.[712] The Mahistan (Council of Nobles[713] and priests[714]) "was similar to the Roman Senate, its members being responsible for drawing laws and enacting them."[715] These were two different councils, one for kinsmen and one for magi.[716] The various parts of the Parthian realm were ruled "by great noble families, allegedly seven in all, such as the Surens, Karens, and Mihrans. These families commanded detachments of the army in battle and wielded semi-dependent suzerainty in their fiefdoms. The 'king of kings' was such in fact as well as in title, for he dealt apparently as primus inter pares with powerful lords."[717] "The court system of the Arsacids was copied from the Achaemenid model, being staffed by many offices called Diwans, responsible for record-keeping, communication, budgeting, and taxation. These were headed by their respective Dibirs who were all responsible to a first-minister, a member of the nobility as mentioned before."[718] "It seems, however, that the Parthians, much like their Achaemenid predecessors, did not have one single capital and moved from city to city along with their administration."[719] The powerful families that constituted the Parthian military aristocracy would have been able to constraint the executive. Elite families such as the Surens and Karens had the greatest influence and probably held top posts such as "satrap of satraps" and were regular satraps.[720] The reason why they held the top posts was presumably because there would have been unwanted consequences for the king had they not have been able to retain them. The interests of the elite families were not always identical with that of the king's. "As the nobility settled down, and intermarried with the old upper classes, local loyalties took the place of former dynastic ones."[721]
♠ Impeachment ♣ inferred absent ♥ The Parthians were "a military aristocracy, which found it most convenient to rule not directly but through various kinds of authorities". This meant "while provincial administration was extensive, central government was scant."[722] "The Parthian government consisted of the king and the royal court, in addition to the Council of the Nobles (the Mahistan)"[723], which made use of trained Greek scholars.[724] The Mahistan (Council of Nobles[725] and priests[726]) "was similar to the Roman Senate, its members being responsible for drawing laws and enacting them."[727] These were two different councils, one for kinsmen and one for magi.[728] The various parts of the Parthian realm were ruled "by great noble families, allegedly seven in all, such as the Surens, Karens, and Mihrans. These families commanded detachments of the army in battle and wielded semi-dependent suzerainty in their fiefdoms. The 'king of kings' was such in fact as well as in title, for he dealt apparently as primus inter pares with powerful lords."[729] "The court system of the Arsacids was copied from the Achaemenid model, being staffed by many offices called Diwans, responsible for record-keeping, communication, budgeting, and taxation. These were headed by their respective Dibirs who were all responsible to a first-minister, a member of the nobility as mentioned before."[730] "It seems, however, that the Parthians, much like their Achaemenid predecessors, did not have one single capital and moved from city to city along with their administration."[731] Nothing in the literature to suggest a formal process of impeachment existed.

Customary (informal) checks on executive[edit]

♠ Informal critique of executive ♣ inferred present ♥ The Parthians were "a military aristocracy, which found it most convenient to rule not directly but through various kinds of authorities". This meant "while provincial administration was extensive, central government was scant."[732] "The Parthian government consisted of the king and the royal court, in addition to the Council of the Nobles (the Mahistan)"[733], which made use of trained Greek scholars.[734] The Mahistan (Council of Nobles[735] and priests[736]) "was similar to the Roman Senate, its members being responsible for drawing laws and enacting them."[737] These were two different councils, one for kinsmen and one for magi.[738] The various parts of the Parthian realm was ruled "by great noble families, allegedly seven in all, such as the Surens, Karens, and Mihrans. These families commanded detachments of the army in battle and wielded semi-dependent suzerainty in their fiefdoms. The 'king of kings' was such in fact as well as in title, for he dealt apparently as primus inter pares with powerful lords."[739] "The court system of the Arsacids was copied from the Achaemenid model, being staffed by many offices called Diwans, responsible for record-keeping, communication, budgeting, and taxation. These were headed by their respective Dibirs who were all responsible to a first-minister, a member of the nobility as mentioned before."[740] "It seems, however, that the Parthians, much like their Achaemenid predecessors, did not have one single capital and moved from city to city along with their administration."[741] The Parthian king was considered first among equals within the aristocracy. He likely would have received informal advice from members of aristocratic families beyond the remit of the posts they may have held at the Parthian court.
♠ Informal sanction of executive ♣ inferred absent ♥ The Parthians were "a military aristocracy, which found it most convenient to rule not directly but through various kinds of authorities". This meant "while provincial administration was extensive, central government was scant."[742] "The Parthian government consisted of the king and the royal court, in addition to the Council of the Nobles (the Mahistan)"[743], which made use of trained Greek scholars.[744] The Mahistan (Council of Nobles[745] and priests[746]) "was similar to the Roman Senate, its members being responsible for drawing laws and enacting them."[747] These were two different councils, one for kinsmen and one for magi.[748] The various parts of the Parthian realm were ruled "by great noble families, allegedly seven in all, such as the Surens, Karens, and Mihrans. These families commanded detachments of the army in battle and wielded semi-dependent suzerainty in their fiefdoms. The 'king of kings' was such in fact as well as in title, for he dealt apparently as primus inter pares with powerful lords."[749] "The court system of the Arsacids was copied from the Achaemenid model, being staffed by many offices called Diwans, responsible for record-keeping, communication, budgeting, and taxation. These were headed by their respective Dibirs who were all responsible to a first-minister, a member of the nobility as mentioned before."[750] "It seems, however, that the Parthians, much like their Achaemenid predecessors, did not have one single capital and moved from city to city along with their administration."[751] The Parthian aristocracy working through the Mahistan (Council of Nobles)[752] was part of the government. However, the aristocrats could also have used informal channels to influence the king. However the culture implied by aristocrats agreeing to punish a Parthian king through any mechanism is at odds with the observation the Parthian king was primus inter pares with the powerful lords. Disagreements between the Parthian king and the nobles would more likely have broken down into fighting rather than ended in a legal action which would have attempted to re-establish the power of the lords; Parthian history particularly from the 1st century BCE charts the existence of open conflicts between rulers and aristocrats.[753]
♠ Informal constraint on executive ♣ inferred present ♥ "The king stood at the head of the state, but royal power was regarded as the collective property of the Arsacid family ... a principle that led to rival claimants and frequently involved dynastic conflicts."[754] The Parthians were "a military aristocracy, which found it most convenient to rule not directly but through various kinds of authorities". This meant "while provincial administration was extensive, central government was scant."[755] "The Parthian government consisted of the king and the royal court, in addition to the Council of the Nobles (the Mahistan)"[756], which made use of trained Greek scholars.[757] The Mahistan (Council of Nobles[758] and priests[759]) "was similar to the Roman Senate, its members being responsible for drawing laws and enacting them."[760] These were two different councils, one for kinsmen and one for magi.[761] The various parts of the Parthian realm were ruled "by great noble families, allegedly seven in all, such as the Surens, Karens, and Mihrans. These families commanded detachments of the army in battle and wielded semi-dependent suzerainty in their fiefdoms. The 'king of kings' was such in fact as well as in title, for he dealt apparently as primus inter pares with powerful lords."[762] "The court system of the Arsacids was copied from the Achaemenid model, being staffed by many offices called Diwans, responsible for record-keeping, communication, budgeting, and taxation. These were headed by their respective Dibirs who were all responsible to a first-minister, a member of the nobility as mentioned before."[763] "It seems, however, that the Parthians, much like their Achaemenid predecessors, did not have one single capital and moved from city to city along with their administration."[764] The Parthian king could not appoint whom he pleased due to the influence of the powerful families who may well have used both formal (Council of Nobles) and informal mechanisms to influence the king. It is known there were vested interests in appointments to office: "Certain offices even became, with the passing of time, hereditary in a particular group." The groups had "preferential right" to hold the office.[765]
♠ Informal impeachment ♣ inferred absent ♥ The Parthians were "a military aristocracy, which found it most convenient to rule not directly but through various kinds of authorities". Limits on executive power imposed by religious agents[edit]

♠ Critique of executive by religious agents ♣ inferred present ♥ The Mahistan (Council of Nobles[776] and priests[777]) "was similar to the Roman Senate, its members being responsible for drawing laws and enacting them."[778] "We have no information about the place occupied at the court of the Parthian ruler by the Zoroastrian priests, since the part played by Zoroastrianism in the Parthian state has not been entirely clarified."[779] "Parthian respect for the rights of priests and the autonomy of temples in Babylonia."[780] "the strange Parthian law of succession. The empire was governed by a council of nobles and priests, and dynastic change was governed increasingly by the opinion of the nobles, rather than by right of primogeniture. Armenia and Adiabene followed the Arsacid pattern."[781] Neusner (2008) mentions a "council of nobles and priests" so presumably priests as 'nobles' were members of the council of nobles and thereby be in a position to critique the executive.

♠ Constraint on executive by religious institutions ♣ present ♥ The Mahistan (Council of Nobles[797] and priests[798]) "was similar to the Roman Senate, its members being responsible for drawing laws and enacting them."[799] These were two different councils, one for kinsmen and one for magi.[800] "We have no information about the place occupied at the court of the Parthian ruler by the Zoroastrian priests, since the part played by Zoroastrianism in the Parthian state has not been entirely clarified."[801] "Parthian respect for the rights of priests and the autonomy of temples in Babylonia."[802] "the strange Parthian law of succession. Priests as nobles apparently were members of the Mahistan. However, through the Mahistan the priests would have acted as religious agents so we cannot code 'present' for this reason. However we can code present because the Parthians had to respect the "rights of priests and the autonomy of temples in Babylonia."[806]

Mechanisms of Power Transfer (from one head of state to the next)[edit]

♠ Customary mechanism ♣ present ♥ Since Arsaces I "the Parthian state was an absolute inherited monarchy with a traditional principle of passing down the rule to the eldest son of the ruling monarch." However, there was a large degree of variation as Parthian kings also appointed sons as corulers, or might name as his successor his brother.[826] "The king was traditionally elected by the nobles from the members of the Arsacid family, although the succession of the eldest son was not guaranteed."[827] "the strange Parthian law of succession. The empire was governed by a council of nobles and priests, and dynastic change was governed increasingly by the opinion of the nobles, rather than by right of primogeniture. Armenia and Adiabene followed the Arsacid pattern."[828]

♠ Number of successful overturns ♣ ♥ Have no way of reaching an estimate other than a vague range. Expert needed. Not known how many of this list were deposed as the legitimate king as they all claimed the top title, usually 'King of Kings' - or they were deposed as a challenger who broke away in a provincial rebellion. A provincial rebellion would not be coded as a challenge to the throne but here we may have an exception. The Parthians were a ruling aristocracy who gave a lot of autonomy to the regions. Since they had no bureaucratic capital (the bureaucracy travelled with the court) - there was most bureaucracy at the provincial level - there was no central place for a challenger to launch a coup d'etat. A challenger would announce they were King of Kings, usually but not always after the existing one had been deposed, and if anyone had a problem with that there would be a fight. Due to the great distances spanned by the Empire, this involved a lot of back-and-forth and could take many years to reach a result. The opinion of nobles rather than hereditary succession was increasingly important in who was considered legitimate so who was overturning whom is a matter of conjecture. Sanatruces I. Orodes II. Central Bureaucracy[edit]

♠ State-level administrators ♣ present ♥

The Parthians consisted of a "a military aristocracy" over a decentralized Empire of many peoples whom already had possession of extensive administration inherited from earlier states which the Parthians did not destroy.[843] The Parthians had some centralized institutions themselves but these were not based at a capital city but "moved from city to city along with their administration."[844] The state bureaucracy was a royal court bureaucracy "copied from the Achaemenid model, being staffed by many offices called Diwāns, responsible for record-keeping, communication, budgeting, and taxation."[845] The Diwans "were headed by their respective Dibirs who were all responsible to a first-minister, a member of the nobility".[846] Highly-literate administrators were regularly recruited from the pool of talent that existed within the territory of the Empire, particularly Hellenistic Babylonia.[847] So much so, the official language of Parthian administration was Greek[848], although Middle Persian may also have been in use[849] and the azat (free people) who represented "descendents of the nomadic aristocracy of Parni ... filled the top posts in the state administration."[850] While the apparently peripatetic bureaucracy "is very poorly documented"[851] there were administrative achievements in the form of public works in Parthian regions, such as the road network which the Arsacids maintained and expanded "to the north-east to include their major cities of Rhagae and Nisa."[852]

[853]

♠ Prestige of state-level bureaucracy ♣ lower ♥

The Parthians were "a military aristocracy, which found it most convenient to rule not directly but through various kinds of authorities". This meant "while provincial administration was extensive, central government was scant."[854] The Parthians had some centralized institutions but these were not based at a capital city but "moved from city to city along with their administration."[855] The state bureaucracy was a royal court bureaucracy "copied from the Achaemenid model".[856] Additionally at the top of the Parthian state there was a Council of the Nobles (the Mahistan)"[857] The Mahistan (Council of Nobles[858] and priests[859]) "was similar to the Roman Senate, its members being responsible for drawing laws and enacting them."[860] There were two different councils, one for kinsmen and one for magi.[861]

At the central government the Parthian aristocracy had more prestige than the administrators in the royal court bureaucracy because the Mahistan had an important role in lawmaking and because it was Parthian, whereas the bureaucracy would have contained non-Parthians such as Greeks. There also would have been a military and possibly religious aspect to this prestige since the Mahistan contained generals and priests. Furthermore within the Parthian Empire the royal court bureaucracy was probably less prestigious than the aristocrats because the majority of administrative and governance activity was legally conducted at the level of the provinces and cities, which were given a lot of autonomy (even rights enshrined in charter[862]). Compounding this the royal court bureaucracy was seen more as the personal organ of a Parthian king than an indispensable institution of state. "As the nobility settled down, and intermarried with the old upper classes, local loyalties took the place of former dynastic ones."[863]

Authority over state-level administrators

♠ Direct ♣ present ♥ The Parthians had some centralized institutions themselves but these were not based at a capital city but "moved from city to city along with their administration."[864] The state bureaucracy was a royal court bureaucracy "copied from the Achaemenid model, being staffed by many offices called Diwāns, responsible for record-keeping, communication, budgeting, and taxation."[865] The Diwans "were headed by their respective Dibirs who were all responsible to a first-minister, a member of the nobility".[866] Given the close association of the Parthian bureaucracy to the king - that is, within his court, his authority over them would have been direct.

Local-level officials (provincial, regional, civic administration)[edit]

♠ Number of regional administrative units ♣ 18: 41-100 CE ♥

"In the first century CE, Parthia was divided into eighteen satrapies".[889] "Part of the kingdom was divided into satrapies ruled by satraps appointed by the king. The rest consisted of vassal kingdoms."[890]

♠ Local-level administrators ♣ present ♥

Parthain provinces known as satrapi were ruled by governors who were the top administrator and the highest military official.[891] "In the first century CE, Parthia was divided into eighteen satrapies".[892] Elite families such as the Surens and Karens had the greatest influence and probably held top posts such as "satrap of satraps" and were regular satraps.[893] "In some cases power over a number of satrapies (usually along the frontiers) was concentrated in the hands of the same person."[894] "The various parts of their realm rule by great noble families, allegedly seven in all, such as the Surens, Karens, and Mihrans. These families commanded detachments of the army in battle and wielded semi-dependent suzerainty in their fiefdoms." [895] The provincial administration was "extensive" and staffed by specialized officials at multiple levels who were responisble for the collection of duties and taxes and supervised local communities and administrative units.[896] The well-developed nature of the provincial bureaucracy is attested by ostraca from Parthian Nisa and also Parthian parchments and ostraca recovered at Dura-Europos.[897]

However, the Parthians were "a military aristocracy, which found it most convenient to rule not directly but through various kinds of authorities, each appropriate to the area governed, to whom they maintained a feudal relationship and to whose subjects they desired little direct relationship at all."[898] This lead to "provincial government ... divided into two systems. The first was the system of the client kingdoms and the second, provinces or satrapis ran much like the Achaemenid model. The client kingdoms of the Arsacid Empire, mainly clustered in the western and southern portions of the empire, were ruled by local dynasties and were considered personal clients of the Arsacid king. These had a high degree of internal autonomy and administrative freedom, often being only responsible for paying an annual tribute to the court."[899] Their "normal tendency [was] to preserve local administrative practices in the conquered area"[900] "Source information suggests that typically they used for their own administrative purposes any organization structures already existing in captured territories. In this respect, the Arsacids pursued a policy resembling that of the Seleucids, who built their administration on Achaemenid structures."[901] The geography of the provincial administration of the Parthian Empire can be divided into three areas: self governing kingdoms in Mesopotamia; semi-independent kingdoms on the borders of Iran); and Parthian 'heartlands' in the centre under rule of the King.[902] Greek city-states "became increasingly oligarchical, popular assemblies declined in significance, and power was concentrated in the hands of a council made up of representatives of a few of the richest families."[903]

Authority over local-level administrators

♠ Direct ♣ [absent; present]: 41-226 CE ♥ There is no data available on what administration was like in cities that were established by Parthians.[904] Nisa/Mithradatocarta, Hecatompylos, Vologasocerta and Ctesiphon are examples of cities founded by the Parthians.[905] The Parthians were "a military aristocracy, which found it most convenient to rule not directly but through various kinds of authorities, each appropriate to the area governed, to whom they maintained a feudal relationship and to whose subjects they desired little direct relationship at all."[906] "Different from the client kingdoms were the satrapis, ruled by governors appointed by the central state. We can only guess that initially, these satraps were fully responsible to the Emperor. However, with the increasing decentralization of the Arsacid state and the ever increasing power of the local land-owners and members of the Arsacid nobility, the government of the satrapi was also reduced to a form of vassal rulership, although probably with a more strict taxation system that could be more easily redrawn in comparison with that of the client kingdoms."[907] We do know that while the apparently peripatetic bureaucracy "is very poorly documented"[908] there were administrative achievements in the form of public works in Parthian regions, such as the road network which the Arsacids maintained and expanded "to the north-east to include their major cities of Rhagae and Nisa."[909] While the central government wasn't extensive and direct rule wasn't normally sought a Parthian king may have at times exercised direct authority as and when required, for example to found new cities or ensure building projects or public works were carried out. Direct rule would have been possible due to the fact the bureaucracy moved with the king's court. Where the king went his bureaucracy was there to ensure his orders were carried out.
♠ Indirect ♣ inferred present ♥ In smaller cities (i.e. non-Parthian ones) the king's local officials had the authority to "interfere in cities' internal affairs, even down to elections of city administrators."[910]
♠ Diffused ♣ inferred present ♥ "Seleucid and Parthian cities were self-governing and controlled considerable territories independent of the central government."[911] Seleucia had full autonomy from c.142-43 BCE. The Parthians were suzerains by treaty.[912] In Babylonia "[t]he fully privileged aristocracy formed a religious and municipal commune enjoying a measure of self-rule. These towns owned a land district."[913] "The Greek city-states in Parthia were a survival from the Seleucid period. Under the Parthians they formally retained their autonomy".[914] "Parthian rulers sometimes appointed members of local elites as officials in newly conquered lands."[915] The Parthians provided the autonomous city of Susa with its own charter.[916]
♠ Mixed ♣ inferred present ♥ "the governor of the western frontier of Parthia was a man of very high rank (possibly a Suren ...) and one whose duties included at least nominal authority over Arabian tribes within or along the Parthian frontier."[917] "Under the reign of Vologases and that of his successor, Pacorus II, Parthian interest grew in the lands of Choresmia, Bactria, and the Hindu Kush. The presence of Parthian elements in the coinage of various political centers in those lands attests to the influences both rulers had in the area."[918] Parthian rulers practiced dynastic unions and placed members of family on the thrones of dependent states.[919]

Legal System[edit]

♠ Multiple legal systems ♣ present ♥ "Since the Parthian Empire was an essentially multicultural society, identifying a uniform culture in its territories would be a rather impossible task. The decentralized policies of the Arsacids meant that much of the empire was left to keep its own culture, and often even their languages."[928]

Characene

Southern mesopotamia. capital Charax.
Virtual intependence in the first century CE.[929]

Elymais

Susiana region. Capital Susa. Independent 25-225 CE.[930]
"as late as the first century c.e. Susa was a free Hellenistic city, not under the satrapy of Elymais, but directly under the shahanshan, and from an extant letter of Ardavan III, we know that the Arsacid chancellery in Ctesiphon, as well as the Susa polis, continued to use Greek and to follow good Seleucid style."[931] "The advent of the Parthians did not mark a break in the cultural history of the Greek cities, which retained their constitutions and magistrates, their schools, language, and law, long after the decline of Seleucid power."[932]

Seleucia on the Tigris

The Parthians provided the autonomous city of Susa with its own charter.[933] Greek city of Seleucia on the Tigris had an exceptional degree of autonomy and under its legal status minted its own coinage.[934] Seleucia had full autonomy from c.142-43 BCE. The Parthians were suzerains by treaty.[935] Seleucia began to decline after founding of Vologasias.[936] "The advent of the Parthians did not mark a break in the cultural history of the Greek cities, which retained their constitutions and magistrates, their schools, language, and law, long after the decline of Seleucid power."[937]

Dura

preserved Greek language and political forms.[938] "The advent of the Parthians did not mark a break in the cultural history of the Greek cities, which retained their constitutions and magistrates, their schools, language, and law, long after the decline of Seleucid power."[939]

Jewish community

Due to their political usefulness vis-a-vis the Romans and the Greeks "the Jewish population obtained the right to have a government of its own."[940] "Ruled by their exilarch, the Jews were largely left to manage their own affairs based on their laws, conforming in general sense with the Parthian state laws and often encouraging the Jewish population to follow the local laws as closely as the Old Testament ones."[941]

Babylon

"In 91 b.c.e. Gotarzes I appeared as independent ruler of Babylonia, although all we know for certain is that he was recognized as king in Babylon. The further extent of his authority is not known. At any rate, as a contender for the throne, he remained in control of the area after Mithridates' death in 87. ... Gotarzes held Babylonia until 81/80, when the territory passed into the hands of a new rebel, Orodes I."[942] Babylonia "was not touched by the Romans in their invasion of 54-3, or by Antony in 39-31 during his unsuccessful Armenian adventure."[943] "The Babyonians were doubtless offended by the Seleucid's parallel policy of Hellenization, and the creation of a center of Hellenism at Babylon could not have pleased the priests and tradesmen there."[944] "The Babylonian for the most part preserved his civilization, language, and religion unmodified by Greece or Iran. His was the law in western Asia which was least touched by Greek law, and that law still governed internal commerce."[945] However, Babylon was abandonded during the period 300 BCE - 1st century CE

♠ Property can be owned by people from all social classes ♣ ♥

Social Mobility[edit]

Status[edit]

Elite status

♠ elite status is hereditary ♣ present ♥ "It was status as an agnate [kin group or clan] in one of the noble groups that alone gave access to appointment to any state or court official of importance. Certain offices even became, with the passing of time, hereditary in a particular group." The groups had "preferential right" to hold the office.[973] Elite families such as the Surens and Karens had the greatest influence and probably held top posts such as "satrap of satraps" and were regular satraps.[974]
♠ elite status is selected ♣ present ♥ Highly-literate administrators were regularly recruited from the pool of talent that existed within the territory of the Empire, particularly Hellenistic Babylonia.[975] So much so, the official language of Parthian administration was Greek[976], although Middle Persian may also have been in use.[977]
♠ formal system of hereditary social status ♣ ♥ "It was status as an agnate [kin group or clan] in one of the noble groups that alone gave access to appointment to any state or court official of importance. Certain offices even became, with the passing of time, hereditary in a particular group." The groups had "preferential right" to hold the office.[978]

♠ social mobility ♣ inferred absent ♥ "It was status as an agnate [kin group or clan] in one of the noble groups that alone gave access to appointment to any state or court official of importance. Certain offices even became, with the passing of time, hereditary in a particular group." The groups had "preferential right" to hold the office.[979] Elite families such as the Surens and Karens had the greatest influence and probably held top posts such as "satrap of satraps" and were regular satraps.[980] "It is assumed that native inhabitants of Parthia, who made up the lower social orders, still stood higher in the social hierarchy than their equivalents in Parthian-conquered lands."[981]

♠ recruitment of chief executive ♣ equal ♥ The 'king of kings' was such in fact as well as in title, for he dealt apparently as primus inter pares with powerful lords."[982] "The empire was governed by a council of nobles and priests, and dynastic change was governed increasingly by the opinion of the nobles, rather than by right of primogeniture. Armenia and Adiabene followed the Arsacid pattern."[983]
♠ extreme status elevation ♣ absent ♥
♠ extreme status diminution ♣ ♥

♠ occupational mobility ♣ present ♥ "In the cities, the laborers also paid their head-taxes and other levies from their wage labor while having the freedom to chose alternate occupations and movement within the empire."[984]

Slavery[edit]

♠ Proportion of population enslaved ♣ [10-25] ♥ Perhaps in Greek run cities but not in territories directly run by the Parthians, except for war captives.

"Although slaves must have constituted a large social group, little can be discerned about their place and role in the Parthian society."[985]

"In the eastern regions, the switch to a class society only took place at the beginning of the first millennium B.C. In that and particularly in the Achaemenid period, slavery became a widespread practice in the eastern Iranian regions." [986] However, not in the Parthian regions: the lower class Parni were "dependent on the Parni aristocracy."[987] The sedentary agriculturalists "were formed into communities, where they personally enjoyed full legal rights. They owned specific property, which they could buy and sell. But they were not considered entirely free, and there was a clear distinction between them and the upper class. They depended on the ruling class, and that depedence was collective. ... in no case could they obtain full personal freedom. Exploitation of that social class was apparent and primarily took the form of payment of taxes."[988] This exploitation is not strictly slavery.

"Written sources do not seem to indicate the existence of slavery outside military slavery in the Parthian lands. War captives were often used as free labor and there are several mentions of major construction projects being undertaken using war captives."[989]

"In the cities, the laborers also paid their head-taxes and other levies from their wage labor while having the freedom to choose alternate occupations and movement within the empire."[990]

However, slavery surely was present in the Greek city Seleucia on the Tigris - and at Susa.

"The major slave-holding centres were the Greek city-states. Manumission records found in Susa reflect the considerable extent of ancient slavery."[991]

♠ Proportion of population enserfed ♣ 0 ♥ "The Parthian society was essentially divided into two division of land-holding and landless population."[992] "Small land-owners were consisted of village chiefs and petty farmers. These usually wielded much local power and often acted as agents of the nobility in managing their lands as well. Land was thus rented to the peasants who were entitled to the products of their labor and had to pay a rent for the use of the agricultural land."[993] "In general, the populations of the rural and agricultural areas were the semi-land owning or tenant farmers who produced various agricultural products and used them to pay rents while keeping their claims to the land and guaranteeing themselves a freedom of movement and sale of excess products."[994] Were rural peasants legally 'bound to the land' which is the definition of serfdom or merely renting it from the lords? Seems like they were 'guaranteed freedom of movement' so were not bound. "In the cities, the laborers also paid their head-taxes and other levies from their wage labor while having the freedom to choose alternate occupations and movement within the empire."[995] It seems as though it would have been possible for a landless peasant to move to a city and pursue an alternative occupation there.

In Parthian regions the sedentary agriculturalists "were formed into communities, where they personally enjoyed full legal rights. They owned specific property, which they could buy and sell. But they were not considered entirely free, and there was a clear distinction between them and the upper class. They depended on the ruling class, and that depedence was collective. ... in no case could they obtain full personal freedom. Exploitation of that social class was apparent and primarily took the form of payment of taxes."[996]

War captives were often used as free labor and there are several mentions of major construction projects being undertaken using war captives."[998] However, slavery was surely present in the Greek city Seleucia on the Tigris.

There is no mention of peasants being recruited as corvee labour for construction projects.

Religion and Normative Ideology[edit]

♠ RA ♣ Enrico Cioni ♥

♠ Expert ♣ ♥

Description of the Normative Ideology

Deification of Rulers[edit]

♠ Rulers are legitimated by gods ♣ inferred present ♥ Inferred from the fact that rulers were legitimated by the gods both among the Achaemenids and among the Sasanians. Schmitt says that although the Achaemenid kings were not gods or descendants of gods, 'the other fundamental basis of their kingship beside the genealogical principle is the theory of divine right of kings, Gottesgnadentum. They are kings vasna Auramazdaha, "by the favor of Ahura Mazda;" [...]. This supreme god "bestowed the empire" (xsacam frabara) on the kings. As these and similar expressions show, the Achaemenids' reign is legitimized by the gods, and the king is invested by them; i.e., he is their elect and their representative on earth [...]' [1,018] As for the Sasanians: "As seen on Ardashir’s investiture relief above, for Sasanian monarchs it was all about the xwarrah, the divine fortune or glory, which was brought to the king, and then to the land and its inhabitants through the bestowal of power by Ohrmazd." [1,019] Of course, it is likely that the Parthians practiced Zoroastrianism in a somewhat different way from the other two Zoroastrian dynasties, but it also seems reasonable to infer some degree of continuity.

♠ Rulers control supernatural energy ♣ inferred absent ♥ Inferred from the fact that, for both the Achaemenids [1,020] and the Sasanians [1,021], the rulers benefited from supernatural energy (known as xwarrah, farnah and other variants), but did not control it--Ahura Mazda, the Zoroastrian god, did. Of course, it is likely that the Parthians practiced Zoroastrianism in a somewhat different way from the other two Zoroastrian dynasties, but it also seems reasonable to infer some degree of continuity. Of course, it is likely that the Parthians practiced Zoroastrianism in a somewhat different way from the other two Zoroastrian dynasties, but it also seems reasonable to infer some degree of continuity.

♠ Rulers are descendants of gods ♣ inferred absent ♥ Inferred from the fact that this was not believed to be the case either among the Achaemenids or among the Sasanians. "The Achaemenid kings were no gods ... and they were not of divine origin." [1,022] As for the Sasanians, their iconography and inscriptions make it quite clear that the rulers are blessed by the gods [1,023], but have not descended from them. Of course, it is likely that the Parthians practiced Zoroastrianism in a somewhat different way from the other two Zoroastrian dynasties, but it also seems reasonable to infer some degree of continuity.

♠ Rulers become gods after death ♣ inferred absent ♥ Inferred from the fact that this was not believed to be the case either among the Achaemenids or among the Sasanians. Despite the implication in several Greek and Roman sources that the Persian kings were worshipped as gods, 'there is in fact no evidence that they were even deified after death ... and Darius' inscriptions show that deification did not enter his thoughts. In Egypt a Pharaoh was divine. But in the Near East, as in Greece, the notion was - and for 1,500 years had been - universally rejected as long as divinity was regarded as supernal'.[1,024] As for the Sasanians, though the privileged relationship between rulers and gods is a common subject in Sasanian iconography and inscriptions [1,025], nothing in the sources indicates that rulers became gods after death. Of course, it is likely that the Parthians practiced Zoroastrianism in a somewhat different way from the other two Zoroastrian dynasties, but it also seems reasonable to infer some degree of continuity.

♠ Automatic deification ♣ inferred absent ♥ Inferred from the fact that this was not believed to be the case either among the Achaemenids or among the Sasanians. Despite the implication in several Greek and Roman sources that the Persian kings were worshipped as gods, 'there is in fact no evidence that they were even deified after death ... and Darius' inscriptions show that deification did not enter his thoughts. In Egypt a Pharaoh was divine. But in the Near East, as in Greece, the notion was - and for 1,500 years had been - universally rejected as long as divinity was regarded as supernal'.[1,026] As for the Sasanians, though the privileged relationship between rulers and gods is a common subject in Sasanian iconography and inscriptions [1,027], nothing in the sources indicates that rulers became gods after death. Of course, it is likely that the Parthians practiced Zoroastrianism in a somewhat different way from the other two Zoroastrian dynasties, but it also seems reasonable to infer some degree of continuity.

♠ Rulers are gods ♣ {absent; present} ♥ Inferred from the fact that this was not believed to be the case either among the Achaemenids or among the Sasanians. 'The Achaemenid kings were no gods, in spite of Aeschylus (Persae 157), where Queen Atossa is called "a god's [Darius'] wife" and "a god's [Xerxes'] mother" (here and elsewhere Greek ideas are brought forward); and they were not of divine origin.'[1,028] As for the Sasanians, their iconography and inscriptions make it quite clear that the rulers are blessed by the gods [1,029], but are not gods themselves. Of course, it is likely that the Parthians practiced Zoroastrianism in a somewhat different way from the other two Zoroastrian dynasties, but it also seems reasonable to infer some degree of continuity.

Normative Ideological Aspects of Equity and Prosociality[edit]

(CC: The following variables reference only Zoroastrianism. However the previous polity references a number of religions simultaneously. Check with expert the chronology of religion in the NGA, and simulataneous religions. I have changed some variables to 'inferred', where sources relating to later Zoroastrianism are cited. But generally need to clarify when various religions practiced in the NGA)


♠ Ideological reinforcement of equality ♣ inferred absent ♥ Zoroastrian eschatology held that the potential for resurrection and salvation was universal [1,030]. At the same time, a passage from Shaki's article suggests that in Zoroastrian thought, elites were understood as cosmologically distinct from other social groups: 'The omniscient Mazdean religion is likened to a mighty tree with one trunk (the mean), two main boughs (action and abstention), three branches (good thoughts, good words, and good deeds), four small branches (the estates of the priests, warriors, husbandmen, and artisans), five roots (the lord of the house, the village headman, the tribal chieftain, the ruler, and the highest religious authority, the representative of Zoroaster on earth ...), and above them all the head of all heads ... the king of kings, the ruler of the whole world.'[1,031]

♠ Ideological thought equates rulers and commoners ♣ absent ♥ A passage from Shaki's article suggests that in Zoroastrian thought, elites were understood as cosmologically distinct from other social groups: 'The omniscient Mazdean religion is likened to a mighty tree with one trunk (the mean), two main boughs (action and abstention), three branches (good thoughts, good words, and good deeds), four small branches (the estates of the priests, warriors, husbandmen, and artisans), five roots (the lord of the house, the village headman, the tribal chieftain, the ruler, and the highest religious authority, the representative of Zoroaster on earth ...), and above them all the head of all heads ... the king of kings, the ruler of the whole world.'[1,032]
♠ Ideological thought equates elites and commoners ♣ absent ♥ A passage from Shaki's article suggests that in Zoroastrian thought, elites were understood as cosmologically distinct from other social groups: 'The omniscient Mazdean religion is likened to a mighty tree with one trunk (the mean), two main boughs (action and abstention), three branches (good thoughts, good words, and good deeds), four small branches (the estates of the priests, warriors, husbandmen, and artisans), five roots (the lord of the house, the village headman, the tribal chieftain, the ruler, and the highest religious authority, the representative of Zoroaster on earth ...), and above them all the head of all heads ... the king of kings, the ruler of the whole world.'[1,033]
♠ Ideological thought equates all ethnicities ♣ inferred present ♥ "According to Josephus, Cyrus II had been ‘moved by God’ to encourage the Jews to return home and to rebuild the temple in Jerusalem (Antiquities 11.1.1- 2). This perception of Ancient Persian rule as instrumental in the unfolding of Jewish religious history had endured well into the Parthian period, where such beneficence contrasted sharply with the destruction wrought by the Romans. Jewish chronicles from the Middle Ages mention the Parthian period as one of the best in their history, during which the Jews maintained close and positive contacts with the ruling Iranians." [1,034]
♠ Ideological thought equates men and women ♣ {absent; present} ♥ There is disagreement about the degree of sexual equality in Zoroastrianism. According to more ancient Indo-Iranian religious traditions, women could not hope for 'ascent to paradise' but were consigned, along with slaves, to 'the kingdom of shadows beneath the earth'.[1,035] Ketayun Gould thinks that Zoroastrian teachings then 'challenged the status quo by introducing a more gender-inclusive philosophy'. 'Paradise was no longer the exclusive domain of men who had the means to achieve it'.[1,036] Similarly, 'Mary Boyce and Albert de Jong argued that women were generally held in high esteem and occupied a dignified position in Zoroastrianism, their negative portrayal within the same tradition being only isolated and occasional occurrences and in any case inconsistent with Zoroastrian teaching'.[1,037] However, Jamsheed Choksy's book 'Evil, Good, and Gender' challenged this widespread opinion, pulling together malign images of women and femininity (e.g. female demons) from a variety of Zoroastrian texts and arguing that these symbolic structures would have had direct implications for women's daily lives.[1,038] Rose describes how Zoroastrian concerns with ritual purity made 'all women of childbearing age' (along with male priests and male corpse-bearers) into 'liminal figures'; their 'lives have also been constantly circumscribed due to their biological production of "dead matter" through menstruation or childbirth'.[1,039] The position of women in this religious tradition remains 'paradoxical' because 'In the past, the notion of gender equality in terms of the spiritual potential of women has been tempered by the regular exclusion of women from the ritual domain, due to their particular physiology'.[1,040]
♠ Ideological thought equates people of all locations ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Ideological thought distinguishes rulers and commoners ♣ present ♥ A passage from Shaki's article suggests that in Zoroastrian thought, elites were understood as cosmologically distinct from other social groups: 'The omniscient Mazdean religion is likened to a mighty tree with one trunk (the mean), two main boughs (action and abstention), three branches (good thoughts, good words, and good deeds), four small branches (the estates of the priests, warriors, husbandmen, and artisans), five roots (the lord of the house, the village headman, the tribal chieftain, the ruler, and the highest religious authority, the representative of Zoroaster on earth ...), and above them all the head of all heads ... the king of kings, the ruler of the whole world.'[1,041]

♠ Ideological reinforcement of inequality ♣ present ♥ A passage from Shaki's article suggests that in Zoroastrian thought, elites were understood as cosmologically distinct from other social groups: 'The omniscient Mazdean religion is likened to a mighty tree with one trunk (the mean), two main boughs (action and abstention), three branches (good thoughts, good words, and good deeds), four small branches (the estates of the priests, warriors, husbandmen, and artisans), five roots (the lord of the house, the village headman, the tribal chieftain, the ruler, and the highest religious authority, the representative of Zoroaster on earth ...), and above them all the head of all heads ... the king of kings, the ruler of the whole world.'[1,050]

♠ Ideological reinforcement of monogamy ♣ inferred absent ♥ Inferred from the fact that polygamy was practiced by both Achaemenid [1,051] and Sasanian [1,052] elites. Of course, it is likely that the Parthians practiced Zoroastrianism in a somewhat different way from the other two Zoroastrian dynasties, but it is also reasonable to infer some degree of continuity.

♠ Ideological thought reinforces prosociality ♣ inferred present ♥ In an article that stresses continuities between ancient and modern Zoroastrianism, Boyce describes offerings to the dead and the rituals connected with these among modern worshippers: 'Many of these rituals are accompanied by offerings of food and drink, which are afterwards partaken of by the living in communion with the dead, the soul being invited back to join its kinsmen and friends, not in grief but in companionable happiness. In Irani idiom such observances are for "the rejoicing of the soul" ... Some of the offerings are also given to the poor, so that piety towards the dead, cheerfulness, and charity are combined in a way that is wholly Zoroastrian.'[1,053] Cantera says that 'from its very beginnings Zoroastrianism has developed an ethical imperative of assistance to the needy members of the community'.[1,054] "Records found in excavations at Nisa provide evidence of different types of tax collection ... There are also indications of the existence of special levies for the support of religious activities, somewhat similar to tithes."[1,055]

♠ charity ♣ inferred present ♥ In an article that stresses continuities between ancient and modern Zoroastrianism, Boyce describes offerings to the dead and the rituals connected with these among modern worshippers: 'Many of these rituals are accompanied by offerings of food and drink, which are afterwards partaken of by the living in communion with the dead, the soul being invited back to join its kinsmen and friends, not in grief but in companionable happiness. In Irani idiom such observances are for "the rejoicing of the soul" ... Some of the offerings are also given to the poor, so that piety towards the dead, cheerfulness, and charity are combined in a way that is wholly Zoroastrian.'[1,056] Cantera says that 'from its very beginnings Zoroastrianism has developed an ethical imperative of assistance to the needy members of the community'.[1,057]
♠ production of public goods ♣ inferred present ♥ Balali, Keulartz and Korthals link Zoroastrian ethics to the construction of qanats, an ancient irrigation system, in pre-Islamic Iran. Through the Zoroastrian exhortation to carry out 'good deeds', they argue that the worshipper 'is directed to relieve the poor, to irrigate and cultivate the soil, to provide food and fresh water in places where needed, and to devote the surplus of his wealth in charity to the well-being and prosperity of his fellow man'.[1,058] However, the focus of their article is on the lessons we could learn from 'traditional' systems of water management, rather than on rigorous historical analysis. It is unclear to what extent worshippers of Ahura Mazda in this specific polity actually laboured to produce public goods, directed solely by their religious values.
♠ communal dining ♣ inferred present ♥ In an article that stresses continuities between ancient and modern Zoroastrianism, Boyce describes offerings to the dead and the rituals - including communal dining - connected with these among modern worshippers: 'Many of these rituals are accompanied by offerings of food and drink, which are afterwards partaken of by the living in communion with the dead, the soul being invited back to join its kinsmen and friends, not in grief but in companionable happiness. In Irani idiom such observances are for "the rejoicing of the soul"'.[1,059] "Several feasts and festivals are mentioned in textual sources. Spring equinox or the Norouz seems to have been a prominent festival, although not marking the beginning of the year as in later eras. Other harvest related festivals are always mentioned while the Babylonian Astronomical Diaries mention the continuation of old Babylonian and Mesopotamian celebrations, although these might have had only a local acceptance."[1,060]
♠ morality ♣ inferred present ♥ "The individual is encouraged always to choose that which is good in terms of thought, word and action (1.30.2). This choice is encapsulated in the trifold ethic expressed as humata, hukhta, hvareshta - ‘good thoughts, good words, good deeds’ (YH 36.5, 35.2), which remains a seminal manthra for Zoroastrians." [1,061]

Normative Ideological Aspects of Slavery[edit]

♠ Doctrine forbids slavery for co-ethnics ♣ inferred present ♥ Inferred from the fact that one could become enslaved through debt. "Judging by conditions before and after the Parthian period, the lowest groups on the social scale comprised slaves of various kinds. Perpetual warfare must have ensured an ample supply of foreign captives (*wartak, *anaxsahrik, Arm. anasxarhik [Perikhanian, 1973, p. 435]; see BARDA AND BARDADARi) who could be put to work in agriculture and construction. Slaves who worked the mines of the Parthian king were branded (Perikhanian, Camb. Hist. Iran, p. 635). Another type of slavery resulted from indebtedness or insolvency; a fort of indentureship similar to Sasanian bandag (see below) is attested by a loan contract from Dura Europos (parchment no. 10; Rostovtzeff and Welles, pp. 46-47), in which it was stipulated that, as interest on the loan, the debtor, a peasant, was to serve the creditor, an aristocrat, as a slave (see CONTRACTS ii; Frye, p. 187)." [1,062]
♠ Doctrine forbids slavery for co-religionists ♣ inferred present ♥ Inferred from the fact that one could become enslaved through debt. "Judging by conditions before and after the Parthian period, the lowest groups on the social scale comprised slaves of various kinds. Perpetual warfare must have ensured an ample supply of foreign captives (*wartak, *anaxsahrik, Arm. anasxarhik [Perikhanian, 1973, p. 435]; see BARDA AND BARDADARi) who could be put to work in agriculture and construction. Slaves who worked the mines of the Parthian king were branded (Perikhanian, Camb. Hist. Iran, p. 635). Another type of slavery resulted from indebtedness or insolvency; a fort of indentureship similar to Sasanian bandag (see below) is attested by a loan contract from Dura Europos (parchment no. 10; Rostovtzeff and Welles, pp. 46-47), in which it was stipulated that, as interest on the loan, the debtor, a peasant, was to serve the creditor, an aristocrat, as a slave (see CONTRACTS ii; Frye, p. 187)." [1,063]
♠ Doctrine forbids slavery universally ♣ inferred absent ♥ Inferred from the fact that slaves were a part of Parthian society. "Judging by conditions before and after the Parthian period, the lowest groups on the social scale comprised slaves of various kinds. Perpetual warfare must have ensured an ample supply of foreign captives (*wartak, *anaxsahrik, Arm. anasxarhik [Perikhanian, 1973, p. 435]; see BARDA AND BARDADARi) who could be put to work in agriculture and construction. Slaves who worked the mines of the Parthian king were branded (Perikhanian, Camb. Hist. Iran, p. 635). Another type of slavery resulted from indebtedness or insolvency; a fort of indentureship similar to Sasanian bandag (see below) is attested by a loan contract from Dura Europos (parchment no. 10; Rostovtzeff and Welles, pp. 46-47), in which it was stipulated that, as interest on the loan, the debtor, a peasant, was to serve the creditor, an aristocrat, as a slave (see CONTRACTS ii; Frye, p. 187)." [1,064]

Normative Ideological precepts concerning morality/supernatural beings[edit]

♠ Omniscient supernatural beings ♣ inferred present ♥ 'Ahura-Mazda' originally meant 'the all-knowing Lord', but by Achaemenid times had become simply a name.[1,075] However, the quality of divine omniscience seems to have been retained: Whitley uses Yasna 29 to assert that in Zoroastrianism, 'the universe, man and mind are alike created and sustained by Ahura Mazda, omniscient of past, present and future'.[1,076] Shaki also refers to the 'omniscient Mazdean religion'.[1,077]

Form of Morality

♠ care/harm ♣ present ♥ The Denkard, a compendium of Zoroastrian beliefs dating to the 10th century AD but considered to incorporate much older material, lists a set of virtues which adherents to the faith should cultivate: 'Powerful and resolute endeavor for the sick, the impoverished, and the needy; granting of legal assistance; care and protection and constant eagerness for bringing support to the poor'.[1,078]
♠ fairness/cheating ♣ present ♥ "The individual is encouraged always to choose that which is good in terms of thought, word and action (1.30.2). This choice is encapsulated in the trifold ethic expressed as humata, hukhta, hvareshta - ‘good thoughts, good words, good deeds’ (YH 36.5, 35.2), which remains a seminal manthra for Zoroastrians." [1,079]
♠ in-group loyalty ♣ inferred present ♥ Mary Boyce discusses the strong sense of community among Zoroastrians (she is speaking in a general sense, tracing continuities between ancient and modern Zoroastrianism): 'there is also a striking sense of community, of a bond between all those of "the good religion", united by their common purpose and way of life. This sense of community extends to a remarkable degree to the righteous dead, to those souls "who have overcome for righteousness"'.[1,080]
♠ respect for authority ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ purity ♣ present ♥ "The purity regulations in the central section of the Videvdad reflect the understanding of a material division between ‘good’ and ‘evil’. Discussions about how to dispose of ‘dead matter’ (nasu) so as not to pollute the elements of the world, particularly water, fire or the ashavan, hinge on the distinction between that which brings growth and increase, and that which brings decay. In Videvdad, nasu has become hypostatized as a female demon, [Druj ] Nasush, who pollutes the human body, so that it constantly produces dead matter in the form of effluent such as saliva, mucus and women’s menses. Cuttings of hair and fingernails are also considered as dead matter (Vd 17), and the body of a corpse is the most polluting of all. The ashavan is encouraged always to be on the alert not to let such dead matter near the good creations, and this is why one should not urinate or spit into streams. A female in menses is to isolate herself in a place removed from people, fire and water (Vd 16). Pollution by such dead matter can be removed by cleansing first with bull’s urine and then water (Vd 19.20- 5). The wearing of the kusti is advocated for men and women over 15 years of age as a barrier against the onslaught of evil at both material and conceptual levels (Vd 18.54- 9)." [1,081]
♠ kinship ♣ inferred present ♥ Zoroastrian texts from later periods (e.g. the Middle Persian Arda Wiraz-namag), communicate clearly defined roles and moral duties for worshippers in relation to their family members. '"Righteous women" were those who were acquiescent and conforming, showing "reverence and obedience to their husbands", and abstaining from sin (AWN 13.1-11). Those women who abused or defied their husbands or who ignored their maternal duty to their children would condemn their own souls to hell (AWN 26, 59).'[1,082] Chapter 15 of the Videvdad deals with (among other things) 'a man's obligations to a natural child and its mother'.[1,083] These texts are considered to incorporate older, oral religious traditions.
♠ bravery ♣ inferred present ♥ (CC: Justification and citation needs to be provided)
♠ respect for property ♣ present ♥ Zoroaster's teaching was opposed to the 'taking by force of livestock and other wealth by the strong from the weak and the ravaging of pasturelands, which he saw as disruptions of the natural order'.[1,084]

♠ Moralistic punishment by supernatural beings ♣ inferred absent ♥ Though information is scant, it seems that crimes were either punished in the family or by the king. "In regard to the administration and organizations of justice in the Parthian period, we are uninformed, except that, in keeping with Parthian tribal tradition, criminal offenses among family members—such as capital crimes committed by brother against sister or father against daughter or among male family members—were settled within the family (Justin, 41.3); they were not subject to judicial process in the law-courts. It is also reported by Philostratus (Life of Apollonius of Tyana 1.25) that the Parthian kings sat in judgement in a specially decorated Babylonian hall in which four revolving golden wheels hung from the ceiling. It was believed that the wheels represented the passage of time, reminding the king that, if he forgot that he is just a human being and thought himself to be more than that, he would rouse the anger of Nemesis (goddess of retributive justice). Although the fabric of the story is based on popular fantasy, reflecting the aspiration for justice rather than a royal, sanctimonious expression of probity, it nevertheless attests the Parthian king’s holding court in special cases, such as, perhaps, capital offenses against the state. However, a certain measure of late Parthian legal practice and judicature may be appraised from the judicial state prevailing under their heirs, the early Sasanians, although PArs, even under the Parthians, had her own specific religious and social environment." [1,085]
