Well-Being[edit]

Economic Well-Being[edit]

State Intervention in Economy

♠ The state regulates banking ♣ present ♥ "Banking in Iran dates back to 600 BCE ... Private banks, such as those of the Egibi family and the Murashu family, were founded at that time and engaged in mortgages, deposits, and loans. Customers possessed checkbooks and invested in property, farms, slaves, cattle, irrigation projects, fishing, and commercial ships."[1,093] "the Parthians realized the great importance of Greek commercial and banking classes centered at Seleucia"[1,094] "The Babylonian for the most part preserved his civilization, language, and religion unmodified by Greece or Iran. His was the law in western Asia which was least touched by Greek law, and that law still governed internal commerce. While the Greek system of registration of documents was accepted by Babylonians, Greek commercial vocabulary did not supplant that of Babylon."[1,095] Credit and debt present, and enforced legally.[1,096] Regulations for commerce existed but they weren't directly Parthian laws because they were under autonomous states under nominal Parthian authority. However, because they already existed the Parthians didn't need to introduce them.
♠ Price controls ♣ ♥ "the Parthians realized the great importance of Greek commercial and banking classes centered at Seleucia"[1,097] "The Babylonian for the most part preserved his civilization, language, and religion unmodified by Greece or Iran. His was the law in western Asia which was least touched by Greek law, and that law still governed internal commerce. While the Greek system of registration of documents was accepted by Babylonians, Greek commercial vocabulary did not supplant that of Babylon."[1,098] Regulations for commerce existed but they weren't directly Parthian laws because they were under autonomous states under nominal Parthian authority. However, because they already existed the Parthians didn't need to introduce them.
♠ Regulations against hoarding/stockpiling goods ♣ ♥
♠ Regulations regarding usury ♣ inferred present ♥ Credit and debt structures were present and legally enforced.[1,099]
♠ Debt-relief measures ♣ ♥ Credit and debt structures were present and legally enforced.[1,100]
♠ Pensions ♣ ♥
♠ Standardization of weights/measures ♣ present ♥ Standardized weights and measures had a long history in the region. Standardization of capacity, weight, length, distance in Persia probably goes at least as far back as the Achaemenids (discussion Bivar 2010[1,101]). The standard unit of weight of the shekel "was enforced throughout the Archaemenid Empire by Darius the Great ... around 515 BCE. In this system, the shekel stood at 8.40 grams."[1,102]
♠ Guarantee stability of coin value ♣ inferred present ♥ Parthians had coinage and there was a very large mint at Seleucia.[1,103] In the most economically advanced regions (e.g. Mesopotamia, Susiana, Margiana) a "vast quantity of small bronze coins" were minted.[1,104]
♠ Public markets ♣ inferred present ♥ "Parthian cities included palace complexes, fortresses for the military garrison, and temple complexes, as well as markets and residential areas."[1,105]

♠ Drinking water ♣ [present; absent] ♥ [present; absent] for the Parthian Empire - Second Period knowledge and or infrastructure retained from the Achaemenid Empire era: "Darius ordered the reconstruction of the city of Sarod destroyed by the Greeks. Mendrokles presented to Darius a plan of the city, which was to be built over an area of 50 x 50 Ostad [1 Ostad = 200m]. Piped water and sewers were considered in the plan."[1,109] Fact knowledge may have been retained implied by complex water infrastructure in the new city of Dara: "Tridot or Tirdad, whose foresight rivaled that of Cyrus of Darius built a large city called Dara or Darium or Darius in the year 211 BC near the present day Abivard, to preserve the name of Darius the Great for prosperity. In this city the water flowed in closed conduits and there were provisions for sewers. All houses were equipped with heaters and central heating, which brought steam from a hot water tank to the rooms via a piping system."[1,110]

♠ Irrigation works ♣ present ♥ "Iranians were the inventors of qanats ... during the Archaemenid era there appeared an extensive system of underground networks known as qanats".[1,111] "sites from the Parthian period together with irrigation canals have been found at Qala-i Sam, Kuh-i Khawaja, Sultan Baba Ziyarat"[1,112]

Entertainment and Knowledge Well-Being[edit]

♠ Funding for festivals ♣ inferred present ♥ "Several feasts and festivals are mentioned in textual sources. Spring equinox or the Norouz seems to have been a prominent festival, although not marking the beginning of the year as in later eras. Other harvest related festivals are always mentioned while the Babylonian Astronomical Diaries mention the continuation of old Babylonian and Mesopotamian celebrations, although these might have had only a local acceptance."[1,114]

♠ Entertainment buildings ♣ present ♥ "At least in one occasion, a Parthian Emperor is said to have enjoyed plays staged in a Greek style amphitheater, while this might have been an isolated event. We can, however, only speculate about the means of entertainment in the Parthian territories, apart from the religious and cultural festivals in areas such as Babylon."[1,115] "at Nisa a Greek theater was excavated."[1,116] Nisa was a Parthian city.

♠ Theatres ♣ present ♥ "At least in one occasion, a Parthian Emperor is said to have enjoyed plays staged in a Greek style amphitheater, while this might have been an isolated event. We can, however, only speculate about the means of entertainment in the Parthian territories, apart from the religious and cultural festivals in areas such as Babylon."[1,117] "at Nisa a Greek theater was excavated."[1,118] Nisa was a Parthian city.
♠ Arenas ♣ inferred present ♥ Presumably at Greek Seleucia on the Tigris and probably at other Greek cities.
♠ Ritual buildings/spaces ♣ present ♥ e.g temples or the ceremonial halls at Nippur.[1,119] the royal necropolis was at Asaak (Astauene) which was a city established by Arsaces I.[1,120]
♠ Other ♣ ♥
♠ accessibility of entertainment buildings ♣ ♥

♠ Full-time entertainment specialists ♣ present ♥ Musicians. "Many frescoes and even coins depict various musical instruments, including lutes, hand drums, kettle drums, and flutes. Sources such as Greek historians as well as Iranian folk-tales talk about Parthian music and musicians, while scholars have identified various modern local modes to be a combination of local modes and Greek influences."[1,121] "We also know of the presence of wandering singer-musicians in Parthian Armenia who played short-necked lutes which accompanied their singing of epic tales."[1,122] "Greek served as their official language; Greek drama was cultivated at their court ... Parthian poet-musicians (gosans) preserved ancient East Iranian legends, embellishing these with heroic tales from other sources."[1,123]

♠ status of full-time entertainment specialists ♣ ♥

♠ Access to education ♣ present ♥ Youth of the elite were trained to be warriors: "there are no reasons to believe that there was any requirement of learning sciences for the noble youth."[1,124] "The philosophical and liberal learning was left largely to the scribal and priestly class who in continuation of the role of similar classes under the Achaemenids, were responsible for the every day running of the government."[1,125] Scribes had "scribal training consisting of memorizing endless administrative terms and their logographic rendering, while their practical training as administrators came from their time apprenticing in the royal or provincial courts."[1,126] They were also likely to have been multi-lingual.[1,127]

♠ Publicly funded or subsidized education ♣ inferred absent ♥ "We cannot be certain of the existence of any organized schooling for lower classes and it is likely that the peasant and artisan classes were illiterate. At the same time, the relative spread of mercantile culture might point out to the existence of some sort of schooling, at least in ancient centers of education and learning like Babylonia."[1,128] Schooling would have been funded privately.
♠ Schools for children? ♣ inferred absent ♥ "We cannot be certain of the existence of any organized schooling for lower classes and it is likely that the peasant and artisan classes were illiterate. At the same time, the relative spread of mercantile culture might point out to the existence of some sort of schooling, at least in ancient centers of education and learning like Babylonia."[1,129] Schooling would have been funded privately.
♠ Universities, “proto-universities”, or other higher education ♣ inferred present ♥ "The Hellenistic commercial aristocracy of the city [Seleucia] supported cultural endeavors, as patrons of literature, art, and academies."[1,130]
♠ Libraries ♣ present ♥ "the Avesta (the holy book of Zoroastrianism) ... credits Vologases I (51-78 CE) with the collection and compiling of the corpus of Avesta itself. Whether true or not, this is an evidence of the interest of later Arsacid Emperors in Zoroastrianism and their possible patronage of its spread."[1,131] "Parthian Empire in particular seems to have become a refuge for the Jews who fled the Roman persecution and brought the fundamental of the rabbinic learning with them, to appear in the form of the Babylonian Talmud a few centuries later."[1,132] "Babylonian astronomy, astrology, mathematics, and medicine were studied and developed by Greek inhabitants of the region [of Babylonia], and Babylonian astrology flooded the western world."[1,133] e.g. at Seleucia.
♠ Museums ♣ ♥
♠ Literacy rate ♣ [5-15] ♥ "We cannot be certain of the existence of any organized schooling for lower classes and it is likely that the peasant and artisan classes were illiterate. At the same time, the relative spread of mercantile culture might point out to the existence of some sort of schooling, at least in ancient centers of education and learning like Babylonia."[1,134] "We know that varied schools of philosophy flourished in Hellenistic Babylonia, and Greek metaphysicians, astronomers, naturalists, historians, geographers, and physicians worked there. The Parthian court made considerable use of such trained and able men for building its bureaucracy."[1,135] "Babylonian astronomy, astrology, mathematics, and medicine were studied and developed by Greek inhabitants of the region [of Babylonia], and Babylonian astrology flooded the western world."[1,136] "Parthian Empire in particular seems to have become a refuge for the Jews who fled the Roman persecution and brought the fundamental of the rabbinic learning with them, to appear in the form of the Babylonian Talmud a few centuries later."[1,137] "The philosophical and liberal learning was left largely to the scribal and priestly class who in continuation of the role of similar classes under the Achaemenids, were responsible for the every day running of the government."[1,138] "the Egyptian philosopher Plotinus made a trip to the Parthian lands alongside the Roman Emperor Caracalla and was much influenced by the intellectual environment of Mesopotamia."[1,139] "The Hellenistic commercial aristocracy of the city [Seleucia] supported cultural endeavors, as patrons of literature, art, and academies."[1,140] At least in Parthian held territories there was probably no schooling funded by state authorities. Learning was for an elite and those who could afford to pay for it, such as wealthy merchants. Literacy in the Parthian Empire would therefore have been confined to the higher classes and the merchant class. Literacy also would have had a geographical cline: much higher in Mesopotamia, among the Babylonians, Greeks, and Jews than in other areas of the Empire. The elite of the Parthians were trained as warriors and apparently 'not required to learn the sciences'.[1,141] When the Parthians sought administrators they often recruited from Hellenistic Babylonia.[1,142]

Economy variables (polity-level)[edit]

State Income[edit]

♠ Tribute ♣ present ♥ The provinces and cities outside the Parthia heartlands paid tribute or allegiance to the ‘king of kings’, but regional lords retained their own power. Example Characene? regained c150/151 CE: "With the amount of trade going through that land, its independence was a loss of much revenue to the Parthians."[1,146] "The provincial government was divided into two systems. The first was the system of the client kingdoms and the second, provinces or satrapis ran much like the Achaemenid model. The client kingdoms of the Arsacid Empire, mainly clustered in the western and southern portions of the empire, were ruled by local dynasties and were considered personal clients of the Arsacid king. These had a high degree of internal autonomy and administrative freedom, often being only responsible for paying an annual tribute to the court."[1,147] "At the beginning of the Arsacid rule, kingdoms of Osrhoene, Gordiane, Adiabene in northern Mesopotamia/Assyria, Characene in Babylon, Elymais in Elam, and that of the Frataraka in Persis were identifiable. However, these soon succumbed to the increasing Arsacid power and were reduced to client kingdoms and vassals, preserving their local dynasties as vassals of the Arsacids and subjected to annual tributes."[1,148]

♠ Staple ♣ present ♥ Ostraca texts: "The ostraca suggest that the Parthian kings could rely on revenues in kind from their own estates."[1,149] Taxes could be paid in kind, such as in wine.[1,150]
♠ Labor ♣ ♥
♠ Currency ♣ inferred present ♥ "Mining may also have been an important source of state revenue".[1,151] Also various forms of trade revenue would have brought in currency. "The Arsacids depended in large measure upon their own land holdings and on direct commercial duties, rather than on taxes of agricultural produce imposed by a central regime uniformly on all territories."[1,152]

Debt and Indemnities

♠ Polity has obligation to other polity or group ♣ ♥
♠ Polity receives obligatory payment from other polity or group ♣ present ♥ The provinces and cities outside the Parthia heartlands paid tribute or allegiance to the ‘king of kings’, but regional lords retained their own power.

Taxation[edit]

♠ Degree of taxation ♣ ♥ "Taxes do not appear to have weighed heavily on the subjects. The Arsacids depended in large measure upon their own land holdings and on direct commercial duties, rather than on taxes of agricultural produce imposed by a central regime uniformly on all territories."[1,153] "the decrees and treaties with Hellenistic city-states where an effort was often made by the local population to negotiate and fix the amount of tax."[1,154] The state fixed and strictly controlled tax revenue."[1,155] "Records found in excavations at Nisa provide evidence of different types of tax collection, depending on the category of the land. Two categories are known - patbaz and uzbari. Patbaz was collection in kind for the use of the king. It is less clear what the other category was. There are also indications of the existence of special levies for the support of religious activities, somewhat similar to tithes."[1,156] Mithradates recovered territory in Central Asia and eastern Iran which "opened land routes between China and the Mediterranean world. Since they all crossed Arsacid imperial territories, revenues from duties and taxes on luxury goods being transported became an important source of wealth for Parthian rulers.".[1,157] "In the cities, the laborers also paid their head-taxes and other levies from their wage labor while having the freedom to chose alternate occupations and movement within the empire."[1,158] "Records found in excavations at Nisa provide evidence of different types of tax collection ... There are also indications of the existence of special levies for the support of religious activities, somewhat similar to tithes."[1,159]

Taxes are imposed on:

♠ Land ♣ present ♥ "The basic tax was a land-tax, imposed on the farmers in form of a rent, payable to the local large or petty land-owners who collected and dispatched the tax to the royal court or the local administration. This tax was almost exclusively extracted in kind and was often redistributed locally as well as being used for provisioning the garrisons."[1,160] Excavations at Nisa provide evidence for land taxation: patbaz "collection in kind for the use of the king" and uzbari, the nature of which is unknown.[1,161]
♠ Property ♣ ♥
♠ People ♣ inferred present ♥ head-taxes "In the cities, the laborers also paid their head-taxes and other levies from their wage labor while having the freedom to chose alternate occupations and movement within the empire."[1,162] "In cities, royal decrees demanded a head-tax extracted from all male, working people and from the heads of families; children, women, and the elderly being exempt."[1,163]
♠ Trade ♣ present ♥ There was diplomatic contact with the Chinese. A Parthian general met a Chinese envoy in c.199 BCE.[1,164] "The conditions Strabo describes on the Euphrates frontier (XVI.1.27) may have prevailed elsewhere in the Parthian Empire: local strongmen collected tolls to whatever level the traffic would bear and passed as little as possible on to the royal treasury. Various types of land taxes and head tax also constituted the great bulk of all the tax revenues collected in the Sasanian Empire."[1,165] Mithradates II c100 BCE recovered territory in Central Asia and eastern Iran which "opened land routes between China and the Mediterranean world. Since they all crossed Arsacid imperial territories, revenues from duties and taxes on luxury goods being transported became an important source of wealth for Parthian rulers."[1,166] "Roads were dotted with resting places or Caravanserais ... usually maintained by the local rulers, which provided the merchants and messengers, as well as their animals, an easy access to food, water, and fodder."[1,167] "Parthian merchants made huge profits by reselling Chinese wares, mainly silk, on the markets of the Roman Empire. ... This intermediary trade enriched the Parthian merchant class, and the state obtained a substantial revenue from trading dues. Archaeological finds from Seleucia on the Tigris and written records point to the existence of taxes on trade in slaves, salt and other items."[1,168]
♠ Income ♣ ♥
♠ Production ♣ inferred present ♥ other levies from their wage labor "In the cities, the laborers also paid their head-taxes and other levies from their wage labor while having the freedom to chose alternate occupations and movement within the empire."[1,169]

Taxes are imposed by:

♠ executive or ruling elite ♣ present ♥ "Taxes do not appear to have weighed heavily on the subjects. The Arsacids depended in large measure upon their own land holdings and on direct commercial duties, rather than on taxes of agricultural produce imposed by a central regime uniformly on all territories."[1,170] Direct commerical duties may have been imposed and collected by state officials.
♠ local elites ♣ present ♥ "Local chiefs were also responsible for the collection of taxes, which were often paid in kind, and distribution of supplies."[1,171] "The governor was responsible for the administration of the satrapi as well as the collection of taxes, and he was also the ultimate military ruler of the province."[1,172]
♠ state officials ♣ inferred present ♥ "Taxes do not appear to have weighed heavily on the subjects. The Arsacids depended in large measure upon their own land holdings and on direct commercial duties, rather than on taxes of agricultural produce imposed by a central regime uniformly on all territories."[1,173] Direct commerical duties may have been imposed and collected by state officials.

Type of taxes paid:

♠ Staple ♣ present ♥ "Local chiefs were also responsible for the collection of taxes, which were often paid in kind, and distribution of supplies."[1,174] "Records found in excavations at Nisa provide evidence of different types of tax collection, depending on the category of the land. Two categories are known - patbaz and uzbari. Patbaz was collection in kind for the use of the king. It is less clear what the other category was. There are also indications of the existence of special levies for the support of religious activities, somewhat similar to tithes."[1,175] Taxes could be paid in kind, such as in wine.[1,176] "Parthian merchants made huge profits by reselling Chinese wares, mainly silk, on the markets of the Roman Empire. ... This intermediary trade enriched the Parthian merchant class, and the state obtained a substantial revenue from trading dues. Archaeological finds from Seleucia on the Tigris and written records point to the existence of taxes on trade in slaves, salt and other items."[1,177]
♠ Labor ♣ ♥ No mention of corvee labour or a labour tax in sources consulted.
♠ Currency ♣ present ♥ "From the large number of coins minted by both the Arsacid state and the client kingdoms, we can guess that monetary taxation occupied a position of prominence and might have formed a large part of urban tax extraction."[1,178]
